♠ Original name ♣ Safavid Empire ♥
♠ Alternative names ♣ mamalik-i Iran; mamlikat-i Iran ♥ The Safavids their state mamAlik-i Iran [the "dominions of Iran"] or mamlikat-i Iran [the "kingdom of Iran"]. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_2_IrSafvd]
♠ Peak Date ♣ 1629 CE ♥ Reign of Shah Abbas I (1587-1629 CE). “Shah Abbas I (known as Shah Abbas the Great) has deservedly been considered the ruler who revived the political and military power of the Safavids.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_3_IrSafvd] "Abbās I is universally regarded as the greatest Safavid ruler, the embodiment of the age-old Persian ideal of the just monarch." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_4_IrSafvd] Under Abbas I "military, political and economic stability."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_5_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Iran’s role in the silk trade led to the forging of important diplomatic relations with European powers. The death of Abbas is usually seen as the beginning of the slow decline of Safavid rule; certainly by the end of the 17th century they were no longer a great military force and their administration had stagnated.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_6_IrSafvd]
♠ Duration ♣ 1501-1722 CE ♥ Note: Secondary literature dates the start of the polity a year earlier than we do. 1501 is the year Ismāil defeated the Āq Qoyunlu and proclaimed himself Shah. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_7_IrSafvd] The end date is the same 1722 when the Afghans forced Solṭān-Ḥosayn to surrender to them. He gave the title of shah to the Afghan leader Maḥmud Ḡilzay. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_8_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]1527-1531 CE Takkalu regency. 1531-1532 CE Shamlu-Ustajlu alliance.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_9_IrSafvd]
♠ Degree of centralization ♣ unitary state ♥either confederated state or unitary state. Did the shah appoint and remove regional governors and impose taxes and send them to the centre [unitary]?
♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ alliance ♥ The Safavids were an independent state.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Numerous marriage alliances. For example, Tahmasp had Georgian, Circasian and Daghistani wives.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_10_IrSafvd]
♠ preceding (quasi)polity ♣ Ak Koyunlu ♥ "Having lived under the protection of the ruler of Gilān for five years, in 1499 Esmāʿil emerged from the Caspian region, defeated the Širvānšāhs, and set out to wrest control of western Persia from the Āq Qoyunlu. In 1501, the Safavid army broke the power of the Āq Qoyunlu by defeating their ruler, Alvand (r. 1497 in Diārbakr [q.v.], and then in Azerbaijan until 1502, d. 1504), in the Battle of Šarur, in the Aras valley." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_11_IrSafvd] Core region: Anatolia and Azarbaijan: "A once parochial and religiously ambiguous millenarian movement that had depended heavily on the nomadic elements of the Anatolian and Azarbaijani environments now inherited the dynastic infrastructures of two well-established empires (the Aq Qoyunlus and Timurids) and annexed cities and territories from equally centralized polities, such as the Ottomans and the Mamluks."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_12_IrSafvd]
♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ elite migration; continuity ♥ The Safavids drove out the Āq Qoyunlu from Persia with the help of Qezelbāš tribal forces. They installed themselves as Shahs, princes etc and Qezelbāš were given regional governorships. They declared the new regime to be Shi'i and the existing Sunni population was "persecuted, driven out, or killed". [MAJIDBENAM_REF_13_IrSafvd] Evidence for this is material cultural e.g. new coins minted and texts such as chronologies. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_14_IrSafvd] "the Safavids were themselves the posterity of the Aq Qoyunlu, not only in a genealogical sense, but also as heirs to a tribally constituted military elite". [MAJIDBENAM_REF_15_IrSafvd] Ismail began from a power base in the Caspian region, before moving into western Persia and Azerbaijan after defeating the the Āq Qoyunlu.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_16_IrSafvd]
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ ♥ Solṭān-Ḥosayn surrendered to the Afghans and gave the title of shah to their leader Maḥmud Ḡilzay. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_17_IrSafvd]
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ Turco-Mongol; Perso-Islamic; Shia-Islamic ♥ "chancellery correspondence after 1588 pointed to the Safavid dynasty as the custodian of a complex heritage, which on the one hand recognized ecumenicalism and on the other claimed exclusive divine absolutism and soteriological superiority. This Universalist emphasis was in part inherited from the Turco-Mongol world of the Fourteen and Fifteenth centuries, but we also cannot dismiss the importance of those Universalist empires and world conquerors of the Archaemenian and Sasanian eras represented so faithfully as exemplars in Perso-Islamic literature and art."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_18_IrSafvd] "The 'Shi'fication' and Iraniation' of the Safavid Empire over a period of a hundred years prepared the landscape for a regional split into distinct Sunni Ottoman and Shi'i Safavid dominions." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_19_IrSafvd]
♠ scale of supra-cultural interaction ♣ 2,700,000 ♥km squared.
♠ Capital ♣ Tabriz; Qazvin; Isfahan ♥ Tabriz was the first capital of the Safavids; Qazvin was a later capital. Isfahan under Shah Abbas. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_20_IrSafvd] "In view of the vulnerability of Tabriz to Ottoman attacks, Shah Tahmasp [(1524-76)] decided to transfer the capital from Tabriz to Qazvin." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_21_IrSafvd] Relocated from Tabriz to Qazvin under Tahmasp, and moved to Isfahan by Abbas I.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_22_IrSafvd]
♠ Language ♣ Persian; Turkic; Arabic ♥ Persian used in literature and inscription on coins. Initially Arabic was used on coins. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_23_IrSafvd] Turkic was used by some at court. "Government officials and their servants, merchants, artisans and their apprentices, professors and students, all spoke Persian. Business and preaching were usually done in Persian. " [MAJIDBENAM_REF_24_IrSafvd] "Qizilbash tribal elements and the early shahs especially were more comfortable in dialects of Turkish, native Iranians (Tajiks) spoke Persian and the primary language of the established faith was Arabic."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_25_IrSafvd]♠ Linguistic family ♣ Indo-European; Semitic; Afro-Asiatic ♥ Persian is a part of the Indo-European family. Arabic is a Semitic language in the sub-family Afro-Asiatic [MAJIDBENAM_REF_26_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Turkic has been proposed as a branch of the Altaic family, although this is controversial. ♠ Alternate Religion ♣ ♥Great map of Safavids read Celeste (2016) available online.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_28_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Map of Safavid Persia during Shāh Ismāil's empire (1501-1524 CE). [MAJIDBENAM_BR] The maximum extent of the Safavid Empire under Shah Abbas I (1587-1629 CE). Source: wiki commons.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Safavid period of rule in Persia (1501-1722 CE) was begun by Shah Ismail (1501-1524 CE) and is known as a 'gunpowder empire' due to the now widespread use of artillery and muskets on the battlefield.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_29_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Shah Ismail, whose original power base was near the Caspian sea, began the conquest of Iran with the capture Tabriz from the Ak Koyunlu. He declared that the state religion was Shi'ia and the Safavids were decisive for the spread of Shi'ism in Iran.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]While initially the governing system was "largely a continuation of its Aq Qoyunlu counterpart and its Turco-Mongolian traditions"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_30_IrSafvd] it eventually became a "highly cen­tralized and complex bureaucratic system"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_31_IrSafvd] based at the Safavid court in the capital city. The highest officials of the Safavid court included the Vazir-e-azam (chief minister), Amir al-omard (commander in chief of the army, later titled Sepdhsdldr-e koll-e lasgar-e Iran), the Sadr (judiciary and religious minister), and vice-regent. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_32_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]As a defensive measure against Ottoman attacks Shah Tahmasp (1524-1576) moved the capital from Tabriz to Qazvin, but Shah Abbas I (1587-1629 CE) moved it again, further south, to a new monumental city at Isfahan. Under Shah Abbas Isfahan’s population grew to 200,000.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_33_IrSafvd] The rule of Shah Abbas I (1587-1629 CE) is widely thought of as representing the peak Safavid achievement. Incredible wealth acquired from the state monopoly over the silk trade, was spent on large-scale building works. Abbas also made key reforms to improve the administration and the army.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_34_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_REF_35_IrSafvd] [MAJIDBENAM_REF_36_IrSafvd]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ 2,700,000 ♥ km2. Calculated with Google map distance calculator. In comparative terms note: "The Safavid polity was never as large physically as those of the Achaemenians or Sassanians." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_40_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Ismail expanded towards Persian Gulf, Kurdistan and Iraq. In fact under Ismail the Safavid polity was at its greatest territorial extent, later Shahs could never hold onto such large borders.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_41_IrSafvd]
♠ Polity Population ♣ 9,000,000 ♥ Floor estimates a total population of 9 million. He also estimates the urban population was between 10 to 15 % of this total. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_42_IrSafvd]
♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ 200,000 ♥ Under Shah Abbas Isfahan’s population grew to 200,000 [MAJIDBENAM_REF_43_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Safavid Persia did not have large cities: "Isfahan was smaller than Damascus, Cairo and Istanbul."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_44_IrSafvd]
♠ Largest communication distance ♣ 1350 ♥ km. 1344 km. Distance from Isfahan to Arzinjan (Erzincan). Calculated with Google map distance calculator.

Most other cities were much smaller, with Isfahan, Kashan and Shiraz having a population of between 15,000 and 20,000 people.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_46_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. ♠ Administrative levels ♣ 6 ♥ levels.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. The Shah[MAJIDBENAM_BR] - “At the top of the Safavid administrative and social structure was the Shah. He and his court constituted the apex of a substantial bureaucracy centred in the capital. The highest officials of the court included Vazir-e-azam (chief minister), Amir al-omard (commander in chief of the army, which later titled Sepdhsdldr-e koll-e lasgar-e Iran), Sadr (judiciary and religious minister), and vice-regent." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_47_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "haram women and palace eunuchs"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_48_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. khassa lands
khassa lands were controlled directly by the central court, as distinct to mamalik lands controlled by the provincial administration.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_49_IrSafvd]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
_Central government_[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"highly cen­tralized and complex bureaucratic system"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_50_IrSafvd] "Ismail's bureaucratic structure was largely a continuation of its Aq Qoyunlu counterpart and its Turco-Mongolian traditions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_51_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. vakil, vikalat (vicegerency)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_52_IrSafvd]
"Qadi-yi Jahan's nomination as co-vazir, to be shortly followed by a promotion to the office of vakil".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_53_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. niyabat (deputyship to vicegerency)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_54_IrSafvd]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. vizier[MAJIDBENAM_REF_55_IrSafvd] / Vazir-e-azam (chief minister)
vizier an office below vakil: "Qadi-yi Jahan's nomination as co-vazir, to be shortly followed by a promotion to the office of vakil, is a continuation of those policies that had been effected since the 1508 "palace revolution," keeping administrative power out of the hands of the Qizilbash."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_56_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR] “At the top of the bureaucracy was Vazir-e-azam (chief minister). He generally had a lengthy stay in office and on frequent occasions continued to serve in the office after the Shah who had appointed him had died." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_57_IrSafvd]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. Divan-e-ala (State Council)
"The daily functioning of the state ran by Divan-e-ala (State Council), which included the Vazir-e-azam, Mustaufi al-mamalek (finance minister), Sadr, and Amir al-omara (army chief commander). Financial institution was directly overseen by the Shah and his chief minister (Vazir-e-azam). Except for the Shah, the latter was the final authority of financial affairs." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_58_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 4. Divan for taxation[MAJIDBENAM_BR]

_Provincial government_[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Darughah, Daruga (or beglerbegī) and his deputy (same level). Vali (viceroy of border states) & Beiglar-beigi (governor general of a province), Hdkem - ("ruler of major cities and areas") [MAJIDBENAM_REF_61_IrSafvd]
"Provincial governorships were held by tribal elites appointed from the centre or, as a sign of the inability of the coalition controlling the capital to enforce its writ throughout the realm, retained by tribes out of favour at the centre."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_62_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR] dārūḡa was a police officer or a post analogous to the šeḥna: Jean Chardin 1669 CE said "[E]ach fortress or town has its own governor called darugha ... they are appointed directly by the king and each one has a deputy also appointed by the king indepen­dently of the governor". [MAJIDBENAM_REF_63_IrSafvd] Under the Safavids "the term beglerbegī tended to supersede dārūḡa in the sense of a local governor, as distinct from a police officer"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_64_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. asas and ahdat (guards) and mir sab (night watch)

♠ Religious levels ♣ [5-6] ♥ levels.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Twelver Shi‘ism official faith, established by Ismail.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_79_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. The Shah. The shahs claimed the titles of “representatives of the Twelfth Imam of the Shi'ites and to bear the title of Morsed-e-kdmel (the supreme spiritual leader of the Sufi order)” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_80_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]2. Mulla Bashi "a learned person of high repute ... whom many Safavid kings chose as a close companion, who could counsel them on religious matters and read various prayers for them on different occasions." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_81_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]2. Sadr "the highest religious office of the land, whose incumbent was chosen directly by the king." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_82_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]
3. "the chief official religious dignitary (shaikh al-islam) of the bigger cities". Sadars appointed them "with the consent of the king".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_83_IrSafvd]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
4. Mujtahids. These were clerics outside political power. They gave "fresh opinions on sacred law". [MAJIDBENAM_REF_84_IrSafvd]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
5. Imans - leaders of prayers in the mosques. "Chosen freely by the members of the religious community itself."
[MAJIDBENAM_BR][MAJIDBENAM_REF_85_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"chancellery correspondence after 1588 pointed to the Safavid dynasty as the custodian of a complex heritage, which on the one hand recognized ecumenicalism and on the other claimed exclusive divine absolutism and soteriological superiority. This Universalist emphasis was in part inherited from the Turco-Mongol world of the Fourteen and Fifteenth centuries, but we also cannot dismiss the importance of those Universalist empires and world conquerors of the Archaemenian and Sasanian eras represented so faithfully as exemplars in Perso-Islamic literature and art."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_86_IrSafvd]

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥"Interestingly the manshur the various classes of people and professions allegedly encouraged by Khan Ahmed. An informal taxonomy emerges of regulations and statutes against the personae non gratae in a Shi'ite Savafid society: balladeer (sarf-guyanda), innovators (sazanda), street performers (marakah-gir), wrestlers (kushti-gir), athletes (zur-gar), dancers (raqqas), antinomian Sufis (qalandar), fencers (shamshir-baz), cockfighters (khurus-baz), ram-baiters (quch-baz), ox-baiters (gav-baz), wolf-baiters (gurg-baz), illusionists (huqqah-baz), conjurors (shubadda-baz), gypsies (shatiran), minstrels (mutriban), professional storytellers (qissah-khvanan), as well as catamites (hizan), clowns (maskhari-gan), and infidels (mulihidan)." Khan Ahmed rebelled in the c1567 CE. This manshur was "an admonition and warning" sent in a letter to him "in the hope of avoiding a formal Safavid invasion."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_94_IrSafvd]
♠ Professional military officers ♣ present ♥ Officers in the royal guard were full-time professionals.
♠ Professional soldiers ♣ present ♥ Soldiers in the royal guard were full-time professionals.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Georgian/Caucasian corps (ghulam or qullar corps) created by Abbas I.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_95_IrSafvd]
♠ Professional priesthood ♣ present ♥ e.g. Imans. Under Shah Abbas Shi'i clergy made "key pillar of the state. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_96_IrSafvd]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

The central government employed full-time bureaucratic officials, including chief accountants, comptroller-general and chief scribe. They were paid a salary, although it was not a meritocratic system: some post were hereditary and others picked by the Shah. The government did have its own specialist buildings such as the mints.. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_97_IrSafvd]
♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ present ♥e.g. Tahmasp had chief accountants, comptroller-general and chief scribe. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_98_IrSafvd]
♠ Source of support ♣ state salary ♥“High Safavid officials, particularly of the dīvāns, were paid from toyūl, supplemented by other salary entitlements and special tax assessments, like the rasm-e ṣāderāt, earmarked for the grand vizier (eʿtemād-al-dawla; Taḏkerat al-molūk, tr. Minorsky, pp. 85 ff.). Salary payments and bonuses were freely granted, presumably to ensure the loyalty of the recipients." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_99_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Governors with the title daruga held the post for two years and drew an annual salary from the central government.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_100_IrSafvd]
♠ Examination system ♣ absent ♥ "Succession was typically hereditary for all administrative positions, although the shah could always break the line." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_101_IrSafvd]
♠ Merit promotion ♣ absent ♥ "Succession was typically hereditary for all administrative positions, although the shah could always break the line." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_102_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR][MAJIDBENAM_REF_103_IrSafvd]
♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ present ♥ e.g. the official mints. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_104_IrSafvd]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ present ♥Law and its administration was split into secular and religious spheres. Secular law was formed through the legal code issued by the central government. Religious law was Islamic and run through the shari'ah courts. Court had judges.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Almost all taxes were in direct forms and were collected throughout the country in accordance with the legal code prepared by the central government." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_105_IrSafvd]
♠ Judges ♣ present ♥ "the dīvānbegī, the chief 'orfī judge of the empire, who could intervene in any matter under the jurisdiction of the dārūḡa; once he had done so, the latter could no longer concern himself with it" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_106_IrSafvd]
♠ Courts ♣ present ♥ e.g. shari'ah courts. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_107_IrSafvd]
♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ present ♥ Imami Shi'i jurists (faqīh) "Already by the 14th century the Shi’ite jurists developed an elaborate legal system of private law based on ijtihad, the exercise of logical reasoning by utilizing the sources of the law to form qualified legal opinion within a specific timeframe. The exercise of ijtihad in turn led to the development of an elaborate methodology of jurisprudence, the science of the usul al-fiqh. Some of the best legal minds articulated complex linguistic debates on legal semantics and phenomenological discussions on the authority of the text" (Amanat 2003, 3) [MAJIDBENAM_REF_108_IrSafvd]Debate between Usuli and Akhbari scholars over use of independent legal reasoning (ijtihad) [MAJIDBENAM_REF_109_IrSafvd]

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥ Bathhouses built by Shah Abbas in Isfahan in Qaysariyya bazaar.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_110_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Shahs and regional governors were responsible for commissioning and constructing many buildings in the Safavid period. These included religious and knowledge-related building such mosques and monuments; commercial structures such as bazaars; and infrastructure, including roads and bridge. Shah Abbas is particularly noted for the changes he made to Isfahan, after he made it his capital. "The increase in sources of revenue, the expansion of cities and Shah Abbas’ enthusiasm for commercial development provided a new impetus for progress in architecture and various arts and crafts. The result was the construction of many different kinds of buildings - palaces, mosques, madrasas, caravanserais, bazaars, bridges and other monuments. Shah Abbas moved the capital from Qazvin to Isfahan in 1598 and provided it with splendid buildings, shaded avenues, mosques, bridges, gardens and bazaars." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_111_IrSafvd]
♠ Special purpose house ♣ present ♥ Shah Abbas had caravanserai (road side inns) built. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_112_IrSafvd]
♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥ The Shahs built utilitarian public buildings inclduing caravanserais, bazaars and bridges. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_113_IrSafvd] Khaju Bridge and diversion dam in Esfahan province Iran 1658 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_114_IrSafvd] Bathhouses: In 1543 CE "one Shaykh Muhammad Safi built the Dhul-Fiqar mosque, a nearby school and bathhouse in Isfahan’s bazaar. The inscription dates the building to ‘the days of the caliphate of the most just Sultan ... Shah Tahmasp, the Husaynid’.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_115_IrSafvd] Great many Sarais (caravansarais) built.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_116_IrSafvd]
♠ irrigation systems ♣ inferred present ♥ - a "double dam" was built by the ghulam Allah Verdi Khan in the Shiraz area [MAJIDBENAM_REF_117_IrSafvd] Expanded qanat system started in the Iron Age that brought irrigation and likely drinking water from higher areas. This technology spread through the Near East and into Central Asia, as far as China and some parts of Eurasia. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_118_IrSafvd]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ present ♥ Shah Abbas had cisterns built. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_119_IrSafvd] Expanded qanat system started in the Iron Age that brought irrigation and likely drinking water from higher areas. This technology spread through the Near East and into Central Asia, as far as China and some parts of Eurasia. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_120_IrSafvd]
♠ markets ♣ present ♥ Safavid government had a muhtasib[MAJIDBENAM_REF_121_IrSafvd] (a supervisor of bazaars and trade). Shah Abbas built bazaars.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_122_IrSafvd] "the dārūḡa fixed prices, which no shopkeeper dared to transgress, for fear of punishment".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_123_IrSafvd] The kalāntar of Isfahan, among his duties, "oversaw the bāzār".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_124_IrSafvd] General reference for Seljuk? - Safavid? time period: "The bāzār was usually, though not always, divided into a number of sūqs (markets) in which different crafts and occupations had separate quarters. At night, after members of the crafts and shopkeepers had shut their premises and retired to their homes, the gates of the bāzārs were locked and barred."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_125_IrSafvd]
♠ food storage sites ♣ present ♥ "Caravansaries, where goods were unloaded on arrival and where merchants could take rooms, were to be found both in or close to the bāzārs and on the outskirts of the city."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_126_IrSafvd]
♠ Symbolic buildings ♣ present ♥ Shah Abbas had monuments built. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_127_IrSafvd] Abbas I built monumental structures in Isfahan.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_128_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"In the new capital of Qazvin, for example, Tahmasp commissioned new palaces, a whole complex known as the Sa‘adatabad Garden, with bathhouses, four markets, and the Iram gardens."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_129_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]A c1612 CE palace built in Astarabad had "workshops and bathhouses, gardens and parks, the latter with intricate water-features."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_130_IrSafvd]
♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ present ♥ The central square in Isfahan that Shah Abbas built - the Maydan-i nasqh-i jahan - was initially used for sports; The Shah watched polo tournaments there. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_131_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Gambling and drinking houses. "In 1563-4, following a dream in which he saw the Imam, Tahmasp issued decrees forbidding the tamgha - a trades' tax dating at least to the Mongol period - certain guild taxes, gambling and drinking houses all as contrary to Islamic law, such an edict clearly pointing to the failure of earlier prohibitions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_132_IrSafvd]
♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ present ♥ "The Safavids had a library in their palace in Qazvin. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_133_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Schools associated with mosques: e.g. In 1543 CE "one Shaykh Muhammad Safi built the Dhul-Fiqar mosque, a nearby school and bathhouse in Isfahan’s bazaar. ♠ Script ♣ present ♥ Persian and Arabic script. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_158_IrSafvd] The Safavids had a thriving literature culture based around the court. Persian and Arabic written systems were used. Religious and philosophical texts, history, practical works and, of course, poetry were produced by scholars and artists associated with the court. The central government also kept it own administrative records, although these have not survived.

♠ Foreign coins ♣ present ♥ Foreign gold and silver specie, much of it coming into Iran through the silk trade.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_172_IrSafvd]
♠ Indigenous coins ♣ present ♥ The Safavids minted silver tangas, as well as tumans and dınars. Copper coins were also minted for small dominations. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_173_IrSafvd] When Esmāʿil became shah he had coins "struck in his name". [MAJIDBENAM_REF_174_IrSafvd] To begin with, the basic coins the Safavids minted were silver tangas, as well as tumans and dınars. The range of coins minted expanded over the late 17th century. There were money testers to insure quality control of coin weights and purity. Copper coins were also minted for small dominations, and "From the available evidence it has been surmised that each Iranian city had its own copper mint" although these coins were only used within regions. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_175_IrSafvd] When Esmāʿil became shah he had coins "struck in his name". [MAJIDBENAM_REF_176_IrSafvd] Credit was obtained through Indian merchants trading in Persia. "Whether owing to their exemption from Islamic restrictions on any open practice of usury or to their expertise in money-changing, the Indian banyas (traders and bankers by caste) became fairly numerous in Persia, becoming closely associated with the mints. Credit was also greatly influenced by the multitude of Indian usurers (in Isfahan alone there were over 10,000 banyas in the seventeenth century)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_177_IrSafvd]
♠ Paper currency ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data.
♠ Debt and credit structures ♣ present ♥ Credit was arranged by Indian banyas (traders and bankers by caste)who were trading in Persia. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_178_IrSafvd] Hindu merchants included money-changers and bankers.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_179_IrSafvd]
♠ Store of wealth ♣ inferred present ♥ e.g. the royal treasury
♠ Couriers ♣ present ♥ "The Safavid government maintained a postal system, mainly to relay messages and government orders. The couriers gradu­ally came to be known as čāpār. The system of relay stations no longer existed, however. The čāpārs took riding animals wherever they could find them. They even had the right to make people dismount and give up their animals ... Riding animals actually came mostly from villagers, who would send somebody with the čāpār to retrieve the animal. The čāpārs, of course, did not dare to demand riding animals from important personages or Eu­ropeans."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_180_IrSafvd] Spies used the postal service to transmit intelligence. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_181_IrSafvd]
♠ Postal stations ♣ absent ♥ "The Safavid government maintained a postal system, mainly to relay messages and government orders. The couriers gradu­ally came to be known as čāpār. The system of relay stations no longer existed, however."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_182_IrSafvd]
♠ General postal service ♣ absent ♥ "The Safavid government maintained a postal system, mainly to relay messages and government orders. The couriers gradu­ally came to be known as čāpār. The system of relay stations no longer existed, however."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_183_IrSafvd]
♠ Fastest individual communication ♣ ♥ "The čāpārs took riding animals wherever they could find them. They even had the right to make people dismount and give up their animals ... They wore a special uniform and carried a sword and a stick, with which they enforced their demands on unwilling peasants, who generally fled at the sight of them."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_184_IrSafvd]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ absent: 1501-1588 CE; present: 1589-1722 CE ♥ Abbas I "fundamentally reformed Iran's military, importing Ottoman technology and Western advisers (the Shirley brothers, and others) to set up a standing army comprised of firearms troops supported by artillery with ancillary cavalry. With this force Abbas retook most of Iraq from the Ottomans in the first quarter of the 17th century."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_233_IrSafvd] Georgian/Caucasian corps (ghulam or qullar corps) created by Abbas I.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_234_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The royal guard - the professional corps introduced by Shah Abbas. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_235_IrSafvd] Originally the Safavid armies was raised from feudal levies; these men came from Turcomen tribes rather than from the Persian population. Shah Abbas introduced a professional corps, estimated to be around 37, 000 men in size, including musketeers and artillery. Cavalry remained key to the Safavid armies however, and the numbers the Shahs could mobilize at any one time were much smaller, perhaps around 10,000 men. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_236_IrSafvd]
♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ absent ♥Qizilbash forces did not have uniformity of equipment. "There was no uniformity of equipment or dress: A tribesman brought to the campaign whatever he happened to possess, supplemented by what could be looted on the battlefield."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_237_IrSafvd] Need to check the quote refers to the Safavid Period. The sentence following refers to the armour worn by the elite in the Safavid Period so it very likely refers to the Qizilbash tribes.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The professional corps was equipped by the Shah, feudal troops by regional and tribal commanders. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_238_IrSafvd]
♠ Total army ♣ [90,000-120,000] ♥ This includes both the standing army and the feudal levies. "The total number of the army personnel consisting of the traditional and the new regiments, was 120,000, which decreased to 90,000 under Sulaiman I (1666-94), from which only 10,000 could be mobilised at any one time.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_239_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Savory estimates the total standing army at the time of Shah Abbas at 37,000. This was made up of 10,000 men in the main corps, 3,000 personal bodyguards, a regiment of artillery - 12,000 men and a regiment of musketeers - 12,000 strong. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_240_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Haneda: "the qorchi corps consisted of more than 10,000 men during the entire 17th century” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_241_IrSafvd]
♠ Professional army ♣ {10,000; 20,000; 37,000} ♥10,000[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "only 10,000 [troops] could be mobilised at any one time.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_242_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]37,000[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Savory estimates the total standing army at the time of Shah Abbas at 37,000. This was made up of 10,000 men in the main corps, 3,000 personal bodyguards, , a regiment of artillery - 12,000 men and a regiment of musketeers - 12,000 strong. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_243_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]20,000[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Iran had an available force that seldom exceeded 20,000 before 1600."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_244_IrSafvd]
♠ Largest fielded army ♣ 10,000 ♥ "only 10,000 [troops] could be mobilised at any one time.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_245_IrSafvd]
♠ Maximum logistical lag ♣ ♥ Duration of caravan travel statistics might provide a baseline for this variable since armies were accompanied by baggage trains. Isfahan-Bandar Abbas: 60 days. Qazvin-Istanbul/Izmir 110 days. Qazvin-Aleppo (via Tabriz and Diarbakr): 65 days. Isfahan-Aleppo (via Baghdad and Mosul): 70 days. Qazvin-Archangel: 105-135 days.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_246_IrSafvd]
♠ Sources of recruits ♣ recruits into a professional corps; feudal levies ♥ After Shah Abbas' reform there were professional soldiers. But the 'Qizilbash' soldiers remained. They were not Persian but Turcoman and recruited on the basis of feudal levies. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_247_IrSafvd] "Georgian and the Caucasian prisoners of war, labelled as yolams" were also used. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_248_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Safavid armies were ethnically mixed including "elements of the Ustaiju, Bayat and Dhul-Qadr, Rumlu, Afshar, Qajar, qurchis and ghulams" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_249_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Originally the Safavid armies was raised from feudal levies; these men came from Turcomen tribes rather than from the Persian population. Shah Abbas introduced a professional corps, estimated to be around 37, 000 men in size, including musketeers and artillery. Cavalry remained key to the Safavid armies however, and the numbers the Shahs could mobilize at any one time were much smaller, perhaps around 10, 000 men. The professional crops wa equipped by the Shah, feudal troops by regional and tribal commanders. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_250_IrSafvd] In terms of battle formation, troops would line up, in theory, as "a center and two wings, the left and the right. For battle, an advance and a reserve force would occasionally be assigned. The shah and his non-tribal retinue would always occupy the center. Each of the tribes making up a confederation would have a set position in one of the two wings not only during military campaigns and on the battlefield but also on public occasions such as official assemblies, royal banquets, etc." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_251_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Qizilbash tribal levies to c1588 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_252_IrSafvd] Abbas I (1588-1629 CE) used Georgians and other Caucasians to counter influence of Qizilbash in a "non-Qizilbash military corps".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_253_IrSafvd]
♠ Procurement of equipment and animals ♣ by polity; by soldiers ♥ "Turci-bdsi (or amir al-omard) was the commander-in-chief of the army and had extensive influence over the Shah on military issues. He was responsible for the well being of the army, employment of personnel, support and ammunitions, and superintendent of the royal military workshops (boyutdt)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_254_IrSafvd] The tribal troops must have equipped themselves however.

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥"Between the capture of Tabriz and Herat, that is, in less than a decade, Safavid forces secured territories previously ruled by eight different rulers."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_255_IrSafvd] Ak Koyunlu, Uzbeks, Ottomans, Afghans, Cossacks.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_256_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Portuguese, Mughals, Dutch, Hotak dynasty (Afghan), Georgians, Circassians, Armenians.
♠ Internal wars ♣ present ♥ Decade-long civil war on death of Ismail 1524 CE probably caused by millenarianist Qizilbash tribes, and another Qizilbash civil conflict 1576 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_257_IrSafvd]
♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ present ♥ 1576 CE Civil war. Early 17th century: "the rise of a ‘shadow’ government based in the haram, the growing intolerance of Safavid Shi‘ism".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_258_IrSafvd] 1534 CE plot to poison Tahmasp.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_259_IrSafvd][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Shah Solṭān-Ḥosayn (r. 1694-1722) had real trouble mobilising effective military forces against internal rebellions and external threats. The state faced a crisis of revenues and internal factional fighting among the elite. An Afghan invasion in 1721-22 forced Solṭān-Ḥosayn to hand over the title of Shah to Maḥmud Ḡilzay. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_260_IrSafvd]
♠ Popular uprisings ♣ present ♥ 1592 CE rising of Nuqtavi Darvish Khusraw in Qazvin, 1631 CE rising of Darvish Riza in Qazvin, rising of Mir Uvais. Post-1524 CE Civil war due whole or in part to the "inherent unruliness of the Qizilbash tribal levies." Again 1576 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_261_IrSafvd]
♠ Military revolts ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.
♠ Separatist rebellions ♣ present ♥ "Because the kalāntar was simultaneously spokesman for the people and a member of the official hierarchy he was sometimes able, when the control of the government weakened, to use his power and influence to assert his independence."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_262_IrSafvd] In 1590 CE Abbas I "moved against and defeated the ghulam governor of Isfahan". [MAJIDBENAM_REF_263_IrSafvd]
♠ Wars within same ethnic group ♣ present ♥ Qizilbash civil war. "A 1531 Shamlu march against the royal camp in Isfahan sparked an attack on, and 'massacre' of, Takkalu elements by a combined force including Ustajlu, Rumlu, Dhul-Qadr and Afshar elements."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_264_IrSafvd] Again 1576 CE.
♠ Wars involving groups with different dialects ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.
♠ Wars involving groups with different languages ♣ present ♥ e.g. Pashtun Afghans.
♠ Wars involving groups with different language families ♣ present ♥ Safavid (Indo-European) vs Ottomans (Turkic).
♠ Wars between different religious sects ♣ present ♥ Decade-long civil war on death of Ismail 1524 CE probably caused by millenarianist Qizilbash tribes, and another Qizilbash civil conflict 1576 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_265_IrSafvd] Shia vs Sunni Muslim.
♠ Wars between different world religions ♣ present ♥ Safavids (Islam) vs Portuguese, Dutch (Christianity)
♠ Wars of world religion against tribal religion ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.
♠ Wars on Urbanism-Literacy frontier ♣ absent ♥

The Zū al-Qadar were defeated soundly, and the heads of some hundred prisoners were sent as gifts to the shah, who was camped in Hamadān.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_274_IrSafvd]

♠ RA ♣ Rosalind Purcell; Enrico Cioni ♥ Purcell responsible for most research and coding; Cioni responsible for additional research and coding, and general editing of section.
♠ Expert ♣ ♥ The name of the historical or archaeological expert who supervised coding and checked/improved/approved the result. If more than one expert was involved, list all.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Description of the Ritual System

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Ashura ♥ Procession on tenth day of Muharram among Shi'a. "The most common ambulatory ritual observance is dasta, or procession. There is no doubt that the Hosayn dasta was influenced by ritual parades lamenting the unjust and sudden deaths of such heroes as Tammuz in Mesopotamia and Siavos in Transoxania. The dasta in Moḥarram and Safar developed from simple processions into complex rituals occurring annually among Shi'ite communities worldwide. The most notable feature of the dasta is the self-mortification of the participants in tandem with accompanying cymbals and drums. Marchers chant dirges and threnodies, curse the villains of Karbala (FIGURE 1). Canetti describes the dasta as “an orchestra of grief” (p. 150). [...] When Shi'ite Islam became the state religion in Persia in 907/1501, popular rituals including dasta helped to spread the faith across the Iranian plateau. Foreign residents who spent time in Persia during the 17th and 18th centuries have left very rich accounts of dastas they witnessed, providing a record of the development of this ritual’s pageantry. The majority of popular Shi'ite rituals are conducted in the open, in public spaces, and can be viewed by passers-by. Western visitors to Iran—diplomats, merchants, and missionaries—were fascinated with these rituals and have given us, from the early 17th century onward, quite extensive written accounts of what they saw. [...] A steady increase was observed in the number of participants in the dasta costumed to represent various characters and episodes from the battle at Karbala. Wheeled floats with living tableaux were constructed to pass in front of the multitudes of penitent spectators. A vivid description of the Moḥarram parades was made in 1737 by Thomas Salmons and Matthias Van Goch (pp. 249-53): 'Outstanding in the large public processions are the big theatrically arranged wagons showing scenes of his [Ḥosayn’s, P.Ch.] life and his deeds, his battles, and his death. These wagons are often pulled about accompanied by people, in armor, flags, and emblems of war of victory, depicting some of Hussein’s deeds. For example, a wagon representing the death of Hussein has a deck-like cover coated with sand to represent the arid battlefield. Underneath people are lying thrusting their heads, arms and hands through holes in the cover so they will lie on the sand above to appear as dismembered limbs sprinkled with blood or red paint and colored with a deathly pallor so as to look most natural. Hussein, pallid and bloody, is lying on the other wagon. Several living doves sit on his body, while others are nesting in his blood. After a while, the men under the cover release their bonds, two at a time, so that they can ‘fly to Medina’ to announce Hussein’s death to his sister. Wherever this wagon passes, the people set up such a wailing to show their grief in so many ways and with such conviction, imitation, and naturalistic representations that one wonders at their capacity to give vent to such appropriate signs of suffering so realistically. Their spirit shows through plainly in this impersonation, nor do they spare their bodies: in zeal or desperation, they shed their blood, wound themselves, or are wounded in fights with others; they carry out their intentions with such zeal that it sometimes leads to their own death. It is not possible to tell the difference between feigned dizziness, fainting, and the real thing. One must consider what the stricken people on these tragic wagons endure while presenting their limbs, decapitated heads, hands, and legs in such an uncomfortable position while lying there as if they were dead themselves. [They do this] in all kinds of weather during the processions. Still they suffer rather than move or do something which would disturb that verisimilitude which might be lost.'" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_301_IrSafvd]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ Muharram is one of the months of the Iranian calendar, therefore this ritual only occurred once a year.
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ Muharram is one of the months of the Iranian calendar, therefore this ritual only occurred once a year.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ yearly ♥ Muharram is one of the months of the Iranian calendar, therefore this ritual only occurred once a year.
♠ Duration ♣ 24 ♥ hours. The day of Ashura.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ in absolute numbers
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ Adult men. Inferred from the fact that, in later forms of this ritual, the actors were all men, and often not professional [MAJIDBENAM_REF_302_IrSafvd]. In this ritual, specialists and participants were the same group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ all adult males. Inferred from the fact that, in later forms of this ritual, the actors were all men, and often not professional [MAJIDBENAM_REF_303_IrSafvd]. In this ritual, specialists and participants were the same group.
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ whole polity ♥ "The majority of popular Shi'ite rituals are conducted in the open, in public spaces, and can be viewed by passers-by." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_304_IrSafvd] NOTE: Were women allowed to witness this event? A question for an expert.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Dysphoric elements
♠ fasting ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned by any source, including contemporary accounts: "A vivid description of the Moḥarram parades was made in 1737 by Thomas Salmons and Matthias Van Goch (pp. 249-53): 'Outstanding in the large public processions are the big theatrically arranged wagons showing scenes of his [Ḥosayn’s, P.Ch.] life and his deeds, his battles, and his death. These wagons are often pulled about accompanied by people, in armor, flags, and emblems of war of victory, depicting some of Hussein’s deeds. For example, a wagon representing the death of Hussein has a deck-like cover coated with sand to represent the arid battlefield. Underneath people are lying thrusting their heads, arms and hands through holes in the cover so they will lie on the sand above to appear as dismembered limbs sprinkled with blood or red paint and colored with a deathly pallor so as to look most natural. Hussein, pallid and bloody, is lying on the other wagon. Several living doves sit on his body, while others are nesting in his blood. After a while, the men under the cover release their bonds, two at a time, so that they can ‘fly to Medina’ to announce Hussein’s death to his sister. It is not possible to tell the difference between feigned dizziness, fainting, and the real thing. One must consider what the stricken people on these tragic wagons endure while presenting their limbs, decapitated heads, hands, and legs in such an uncomfortable position while lying there as if they were dead themselves. [They do this] in all kinds of weather during the processions. Still they suffer rather than move or do something which would disturb that verisimilitude which might be lost.'" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_305_IrSafvd]
♠ vigil/sleep deprivation ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned by any source, including contemporary accounts: "A vivid description of the Moḥarram parades was made in 1737 by Thomas Salmons and Matthias Van Goch (pp. 249-53): 'Outstanding in the large public processions are the big theatrically arranged wagons showing scenes of his [Ḥosayn’s, P.Ch.] life and his deeds, his battles, and his death. These wagons are often pulled about accompanied by people, in armor, flags, and emblems of war of victory, depicting some of Hussein’s deeds. For example, a wagon representing the death of Hussein has a deck-like cover coated with sand to represent the arid battlefield. Underneath people are lying thrusting their heads, arms and hands through holes in the cover so they will lie on the sand above to appear as dismembered limbs sprinkled with blood or red paint and colored with a deathly pallor so as to look most natural. Hussein, pallid and bloody, is lying on the other wagon. Several living doves sit on his body, while others are nesting in his blood. After a while, the men under the cover release their bonds, two at a time, so that they can ‘fly to Medina’ to announce Hussein’s death to his sister. Wherever this wagon passes, the people set up such a wailing to show their grief in so many ways and with such conviction, imitation, and naturalistic representations that one wonders at their capacity to give vent to such appropriate signs of suffering so realistically. Their spirit shows through plainly in this impersonation, nor do they spare their bodies: in zeal or desperation, they shed their blood, wound themselves, or are wounded in fights with others; they carry out their intentions with such zeal that it sometimes leads to their own death. It is not possible to tell the difference between feigned dizziness, fainting, and the real thing. One must consider what the stricken people on these tragic wagons endure while presenting their limbs, decapitated heads, hands, and legs in such an uncomfortable position while lying there as if they were dead themselves. [They do this] in all kinds of weather during the processions. Still they suffer rather than move or do something which would disturb that verisimilitude which might be lost.'" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_306_IrSafvd]

[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Euphoric elements
♠ feasting ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned by any source, including contemporary accounts: "A vivid description of the Moḥarram parades was made in 1737 by Thomas Salmons and Matthias Van Goch (pp. 249-53): 'Outstanding in the large public processions are the big theatrically arranged wagons showing scenes of his [Ḥosayn’s, P.Ch.] life and his deeds, his battles, and his death. These wagons are often pulled about accompanied by people, in armor, flags, and emblems of war of victory, depicting some of Hussein’s deeds. For example, a wagon representing the death of Hussein has a deck-like cover coated with sand to represent the arid battlefield. Underneath people are lying thrusting their heads, arms and hands through holes in the cover so they will lie on the sand above to appear as dismembered limbs sprinkled with blood or red paint and colored with a deathly pallor so as to look most natural. After a while, the men under the cover release their bonds, two at a time, so that they can ‘fly to Medina’ to announce Hussein’s death to his sister. Wherever this wagon passes, the people set up such a wailing to show their grief in so many ways and with such conviction, imitation, and naturalistic representations that one wonders at their capacity to give vent to such appropriate signs of suffering so realistically. Their spirit shows through plainly in this impersonation, nor do they spare their bodies: in zeal or desperation, they shed their blood, wound themselves, or are wounded in fights with others; they carry out their intentions with such zeal that it sometimes leads to their own death. It is not possible to tell the difference between feigned dizziness, fainting, and the real thing. [They do this] in all kinds of weather during the processions. Still they suffer rather than move or do something which would disturb that verisimilitude which might be lost.'" ♠ Orthopraxy checks ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned by any source, including contemporary accounts: "A vivid description of the Moḥarram parades was made in 1737 by Thomas Salmons and Matthias Van Goch (pp. 249-53): 'Outstanding in the large public processions are the big theatrically arranged wagons showing scenes of his [Ḥosayn’s, P.Ch.] life and his deeds, his battles, and his death. These wagons are often pulled about accompanied by people, in armor, flags, and emblems of war of victory, depicting some of Hussein’s deeds. For example, a wagon representing the death of Hussein has a deck-like cover coated with sand to represent the arid battlefield. Underneath people are lying thrusting their heads, arms and hands through holes in the cover so they will lie on the sand above to appear as dismembered limbs sprinkled with blood or red paint and colored with a deathly pallor so as to look most natural. Hussein, pallid and bloody, is lying on the other wagon. Several living doves sit on his body, while others are nesting in his blood. After a while, the men under the cover release their bonds, two at a time, so that they can ‘fly to Medina’ to announce Hussein’s death to his sister. Wherever this wagon passes, the people set up such a wailing to show their grief in so many ways and with such conviction, imitation, and naturalistic representations that one wonders at their capacity to give vent to such appropriate signs of suffering so realistically. Their spirit shows through plainly in this impersonation, nor do they spare their bodies: in zeal or desperation, they shed their blood, wound themselves, or are wounded in fights with others; they carry out their intentions with such zeal that it sometimes leads to their own death. It is not possible to tell the difference between feigned dizziness, fainting, and the real thing. One must consider what the stricken people on these tragic wagons endure while presenting their limbs, decapitated heads, hands, and legs in such an uncomfortable position while lying there as if they were dead themselves. [They do this] in all kinds of weather during the processions. Still they suffer rather than move or do something which would disturb that verisimilitude which might be lost.'" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_325_IrSafvd]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Friday prayer ♥ "The Friday prayer is a duty of the community; it contains a short sermon, khutba*, which consists of two parts. It gives some advice usually based on the Koran or the hadith, and a prayer for the ruler of the government. To be mentioned in the khutba meant to be the legitimate ruler. Women rarely attend the Friday prayer but prefer to pray at home.The daily prayers are explained in the Koran as acts of humility and adoration. At the end of the prayer the Muslim can mention his/her personal requests. While traveling or in war or under duress, one can perform the noon and afternoon or sunset and night prayers together. One also has numerous opportunities to perform additional prayers of two or more rak'a each, e.g., during solar or lunar eclipses; when performing the rain prayer; when entering or leaving the house or the mosque—in short, some pious Muslims use every opportunity to perform a brief salat. The belief in the purifying power of ritual prayer is intense; the Prophet compared it to a stream of water that washes off sins five times a day. The performance of the prayer at the prescribed time constitutes ideally a means of educating Muslims to punctuality, cleanliness, and, since there is no ranking in the mosque, equal participation in the life of the community. Prayer can also lead to ecstatic experiences, and when one observes a praying Muslim who is oblivious of everything around him and seems to have drawn himself, as it were, out of this world to stand humbly before the Lord, one realizes best whence Islam derives its vital strength.To perform the salat, a clean spot suffices, be it in the fields, in the train, or in one's shop or office. A small prayer rug guarantees the cleanliness of the place. However, even in the Prophet's lifetime a place for congregational worship was established, called masjid, "the place where one prostrates" (hence the word Mosque). The great mosque, where the Friday prayer is held and which was found in each quarter, is called jami'*, "the gathering [mosque]."Mainly in the eastern part of the Islamic world, one finds small open prayer places called musalla*, and for the gathering of large congregations at the two 'id*, the end of Ramadan and the Feast of Offerings, one sometimes uses an 'idgah*, a vast enclosure with a prayer niche."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_331_IrSafvd]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_332_IrSafvd]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_333_IrSafvd]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_334_IrSafvd]
♠ Duration ♣ 1 ♥ hour. Noon prayer on Fridays. Expert input needed for exact duration. at this time wiki scraper not able to read decimal, so cannot code less than 1 hour e.g. 0.05 for 15 minutes.

♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ priesthood. Religious specialists would have been Muslims, and the Caliphate's rulers were Muslims, which means that religious specialists came from the dominant religious group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ All Muslims, though women would frequently pray in private instead.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_335_IrSafvd]

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Nowruz ♥ "The festive celebration of Nowruz during the Safavid period is well attested (see bibliography). In preparation to it, commanders, ministers, favored officials, rich merchants, and guild leaders were given pieces of land in the vast park of Bāḡ-e Naqš-e Jahān of Isfahan to decorate and illuminate. Each group set up tents with canopies of silk and brocade, and erected booths variously embellished; servants offered drinks and sweets to large crowds for several days. In the royal palace, a large table cloth (sofra) was spread on the floor of the Hall of Mirrors (tālār-e āʾina), and on it were placed large bowls of water and plates of various fruits, greeneries, sweets, and colored eggs. According to Chardin (II, p. 267), in keeping with an ancient Iranian tradition, on the eve of Nowruz people send each other colored eggs as gifts. The shah gave some five hundred of them to his womenfolk. The eggs are encased in gold and decorated with four miniature paintings. The shah sat at the head of the sofra, amongst the royal women he favored most, who were all bedecked in jewelry. They engaged in pleasant conversation, and then, at the shah’s command, female dancers, musicians, and singers entered and entertained the audience. In another chamber the court astronomer was trying to determine the exact moment of 'the turn of the year' (taḥwil-e sāl, that is, when the Sun entered the sign of Aries at the vernal equinox). As soon as he gave the sign that the New Year had arrived, pages sent off firecrackers into the sky, and, seeing this, the household female servants let out cries of exultation thereby announcing the good news to the king and his companion. At the same time, the news was made public by some palace guards firing off their muskets and citadel guards their cannons, whereupon an official band occupying the center of the great town square (Meydān-e naqš-e jahān) beat on their drums and kettledrums and blew into their wind instruments (sornāy). Shouts of joy filled the air; eunuchs opened special bags of wild rue (esfand) and sprinkled seeds into the fire, causing the air to be pleasantly scented. The shah, as all other Iranians, gazed at a bowl of water the moment the year 'turned,' believing that 'water is the symbol of prosperity' (āb rowšanāʾi-st, lit. ‘water is light’) and if one looks at it at the turn of the year he would enjoy happiness all year long. A few prayers (usually Qurʾanic verses, extensively cited by Majlesi, II) were recited, and everyone wearing new clothes drank some water or rosewater, congratulated elders, kinsfolk and friends, and partook of sweets. Elders presented gifts to the members of household, relatives, servants, and friends, and distributed alms to the poor, dervishes, and local sayyeds (descendants of the Imams). In the palace, the shah held a great banquet with wine and music for military commanders, senior civil officials, foreign envoys and notable merchants. In other households elaborately prepared dinners were served, and in general everyone enjoyed the occasion with drinks, music, visitation, and exchanges of gifts and pleasantries. Children were particularly happy, and enjoyed the holidays running around, receiving various gifts, playing various games (specially the 'egg-cracking game,' similar to the children’s game of conkers played with chestnuts in the West), and watching polo, wrestling, and horse racing. The gifts exchanged depended on the status of the individuals. The shah sat in the audience hall and distributed gifts, usually gold and or silver coins placed in small colorful bags, to the courtiers, kinsfolk, household servants and foreign envoys. He received in turn precious gifts from his harem, ministers, representatives of social groups and professions, provincial governors, and envoys of neighboring countries. The usual 'gifts' to the shah included slave girls (especially from Armenia and Georgia, some of whom ended up as royal wives and others were given to favorite officials), money, prized horses, and beasts of burden with precious saddlery (for the gifts exchanged between the governor of Fārs province and Shah ʿAbbās I see Arberry, p. 19). The shah and rich notables also ordered the slaughter of livestock according to religious rites and distributed the meat to the needy. During the following days, people went outdoors and spent the time in the open air playing, feasting, horseracing and, when possible, hunting." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_405_IrSafvd]

♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ "The Nowruz festivities were by no means restricted to the royal courts. Ḵodādād, I, p. 49; for testimonies of poets see Borumand-e Saʿid, pp. 253-384)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_411_IrSafvd]

♠ other ♣ present ♥ "It was precisely because Nowruz was associated from the outset with cultural memories of the splendor and divinely bestowed power of the royal courts of pre-Islamic Persia that it was attractive to rulers, from the Abbasid caliphs to the Pahlavis. Along with its many ceremonies, and most notably that of gift exchange, it provided the rulers with an alternative source of affirming and enhancing their power and prestige through a strictly non-Islamic channel; for unlike religious festivals, they could appear and be celebrated as the focal point and the peerless heroes of the occasion." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_435_IrSafvd]

♠ other ♣ ♥

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Ashura ♥ Muharram procession. "The most common ambulatory ritual observance is dasta, or procession. There is no doubt that the Hosayn dasta was influenced by ritual parades lamenting the unjust and sudden deaths of such heroes as Tammuz in Mesopotamia and Siavos in Transoxania. The dasta in Moḥarram and Safar developed from simple processions into complex rituals occurring annually among Shi'ite communities worldwide. The most notable feature of the dasta is the self-mortification of the participants in tandem with accompanying cymbals and drums. Marchers chant dirges and threnodies, curse the villains of Karbala (FIGURE 1). Canetti describes the dasta as “an orchestra of grief” (p. 150). [...] When Shi'ite Islam became the state religion in Persia in 907/1501, popular rituals including dasta helped to spread the faith across the Iranian plateau. Foreign residents who spent time in Persia during the 17th and 18th centuries have left very rich accounts of dastas they witnessed, providing a record of the development of this ritual’s pageantry. The majority of popular Shi'ite rituals are conducted in the open, in public spaces, and can be viewed by passers-by. Western visitors to Iran—diplomats, merchants, and missionaries—were fascinated with these rituals and have given us, from the early 17th century onward, quite extensive written accounts of what they saw. [...] A steady increase was observed in the number of participants in the dasta costumed to represent various characters and episodes from the battle at Karbala. Wheeled floats with living tableaux were constructed to pass in front of the multitudes of penitent spectators. A vivid description of the Moḥarram parades was made in 1737 by Thomas Salmons and Matthias Van Goch (pp. 249-53): 'Outstanding in the large public processions are the big theatrically arranged wagons showing scenes of his [Ḥosayn’s, P.Ch.] life and his deeds, his battles, and his death. These wagons are often pulled about accompanied by people, in armor, flags, and emblems of war of victory, depicting some of Hussein’s deeds. For example, a wagon representing the death of Hussein has a deck-like cover coated with sand to represent the arid battlefield. Underneath people are lying thrusting their heads, arms and hands through holes in the cover so they will lie on the sand above to appear as dismembered limbs sprinkled with blood or red paint and colored with a deathly pallor so as to look most natural. Hussein, pallid and bloody, is lying on the other wagon. Several living doves sit on his body, while others are nesting in his blood. After a while, the men under the cover release their bonds, two at a time, so that they can ‘fly to Medina’ to announce Hussein’s death to his sister. Wherever this wagon passes, the people set up such a wailing to show their grief in so many ways and with such conviction, imitation, and naturalistic representations that one wonders at their capacity to give vent to such appropriate signs of suffering so realistically. Their spirit shows through plainly in this impersonation, nor do they spare their bodies: in zeal or desperation, they shed their blood, wound themselves, or are wounded in fights with others; they carry out their intentions with such zeal that it sometimes leads to their own death. It is not possible to tell the difference between feigned dizziness, fainting, and the real thing. One must consider what the stricken people on these tragic wagons endure while presenting their limbs, decapitated heads, hands, and legs in such an uncomfortable position while lying there as if they were dead themselves. [They do this] in all kinds of weather during the processions. Still they suffer rather than move or do something which would disturb that verisimilitude which might be lost.'" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_445_IrSafvd]

♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ Muharram is one of the months of the Iranian calendar, therefore this ritual only occurred once a year.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ yearly ♥ Muharram is one of the months of the Iranian calendar, therefore this ritual only occurred once a year.

[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Euphoric elements
♠ feasting ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned by any source, including contemporary accounts: "A vivid description of the Moḥarram parades was made in 1737 by Thomas Salmons and Matthias Van Goch (pp. 249-53): 'Outstanding in the large public processions are the big theatrically arranged wagons showing scenes of his [Ḥosayn’s, P.Ch.] life and his deeds, his battles, and his death. These wagons are often pulled about accompanied by people, in armor, flags, and emblems of war of victory, depicting some of Hussein’s deeds. For example, a wagon representing the death of Hussein has a deck-like cover coated with sand to represent the arid battlefield. Underneath people are lying thrusting their heads, arms and hands through holes in the cover so they will lie on the sand above to appear as dismembered limbs sprinkled with blood or red paint and colored with a deathly pallor so as to look most natural. Hussein, pallid and bloody, is lying on the other wagon. Several living doves sit on his body, while others are nesting in his blood. After a while, the men under the cover release their bonds, two at a time, so that they can ‘fly to Medina’ to announce Hussein’s death to his sister. Wherever this wagon passes, the people set up such a wailing to show their grief in so many ways and with such conviction, imitation, and naturalistic representations that one wonders at their capacity to give vent to such appropriate signs of suffering so realistically. Their spirit shows through plainly in this impersonation, nor do they spare their bodies: in zeal or desperation, they shed their blood, wound themselves, or are wounded in fights with others; they carry out their intentions with such zeal that it sometimes leads to their own death. It is not possible to tell the difference between feigned dizziness, fainting, and the real thing. One must consider what the stricken people on these tragic wagons endure while presenting their limbs, decapitated heads, hands, and legs in such an uncomfortable position while lying there as if they were dead themselves. [They do this] in all kinds of weather during the processions. Still they suffer rather than move or do something which would disturb that verisimilitude which might be lost.'" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_458_IrSafvd]

♠ sex ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned by any source, including contemporary accounts: "A vivid description of the Moḥarram parades was made in 1737 by Thomas Salmons and Matthias Van Goch (pp. 249-53): 'Outstanding in the large public processions are the big theatrically arranged wagons showing scenes of his [Ḥosayn’s, P.Ch.] life and his deeds, his battles, and his death. These wagons are often pulled about accompanied by people, in armor, flags, and emblems of war of victory, depicting some of Hussein’s deeds. For example, a wagon representing the death of Hussein has a deck-like cover coated with sand to represent the arid battlefield. Underneath people are lying thrusting their heads, arms and hands through holes in the cover so they will lie on the sand above to appear as dismembered limbs sprinkled with blood or red paint and colored with a deathly pallor so as to look most natural. Hussein, pallid and bloody, is lying on the other wagon. Several living doves sit on his body, while others are nesting in his blood. After a while, the men under the cover release their bonds, two at a time, so that they can ‘fly to Medina’ to announce Hussein’s death to his sister. Wherever this wagon passes, the people set up such a wailing to show their grief in so many ways and with such conviction, imitation, and naturalistic representations that one wonders at their capacity to give vent to such appropriate signs of suffering so realistically. Their spirit shows through plainly in this impersonation, nor do they spare their bodies: in zeal or desperation, they shed their blood, wound themselves, or are wounded in fights with others; they carry out their intentions with such zeal that it sometimes leads to their own death. It is not possible to tell the difference between feigned dizziness, fainting, and the real thing. One must consider what the stricken people on these tragic wagons endure while presenting their limbs, decapitated heads, hands, and legs in such an uncomfortable position while lying there as if they were dead themselves. [They do this] in all kinds of weather during the processions. Still they suffer rather than move or do something which would disturb that verisimilitude which might be lost.'" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_459_IrSafvd]

♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ 5 ♥ *1524* No contested transition Tahmasp: "The civil war which raged for more than a decade after Ismail's passing did not reflect which serious questioning of the legitimacy and authority of the Safavid house itself but was in the main a struggle between and among Qizilbash tribal elements and their Tajik associates to construct a new hierarchical alignment of those interests around Ismail's eldest son, the ten-year old Tahmasp."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_480_IrSafvd] *1576* Ismai'l II: "as at Ismail's death five decades before, the configuration of constituencies which established itself at the centre over Tahmasp's reign was voided with his death in 1576, but with results which were both familiar - prolonged internal disorder ..."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_481_IrSafvd] "The succession of Haydar, twenty years old at his father's death was supported by a complex coalition ... opposed by a similarly complex grouping ... this latter group captured and killed Haydar and freed his older half-brother Ismail who had been imprisoned since 1556."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_482_IrSafvd] I don't know whether Haydar went through an accession ceremony before he was killed. Not mentioned so assume this is a contested transition to Ismail II rather than an overturn of Haydar. *1578* Mohammad Khodabandeh: Ismail apparently died accidentally from an opium overdose.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_483_IrSafvd] "Soon after Khudabanda's coronation Pari Khan and her Circassian uncle were killed, she by her own Afshar guardian. Ismail II's infant son was also killed."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_484_IrSafvd] *1587* Inferred present Abbas I: "In Khurasan other amirs and troops, including Qajars and other Ustajlu, deserted to other Safavid princes, including the grandsons of Ismail I through his son Bahram. In Isfahan still other amirs supported Abbas' full brothers, Abu Talib, at thirteen three years younger than Abbas, and the younger Abu Tahmasp (b. 1576). The feuding spilled over into Fars, Kirman and Yazd during the third year after Abbas' accession."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_485_IrSafvd] *1629* Safi I: "with no immediate alternatives available and to forestall potential problems caused by any delay in the succession ... [the key officials] ... quickly agreed on the son of Abbas' eldest son ... whom Abbas had in fact designated his heir just days before his death."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_486_IrSafvd] However the throne was still contested by Abbas's son Imam Quli and "the eldest son of Isa Khan, whose wife was a daughter of Abbas". "Imam Quli was executed in June 1630 and a month later Zaynal Khan Shamlu was murdered and Abul-Qasim Evughlu lost his post as chief officer of the haram, both now clearly suspect in a series of plots against Safi."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_487_IrSafvd] *1642* inferred present Abbas II: "Muhammad Mirza - Abbas I's great-grandson by Safi and the Circassian Anna Khanum - was about nine years old when he ascended the throne as Abbas II ... What internal disorder did occur following the accession was, as in the years following Safi's accession, confined mainly to the court and involved struggles for control of the centre." However: "The vizier was then implicated in a plot to replace the sitting shah with another relative. In 1645, supported by the elements of the military ... Jani Khan and Shamlu, Shaykhavand and Ustajlu supporters, with the young shah's approval, killed the vizier."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_488_IrSafvd] Inferred that the plot may have begun within the first year of the new Shah's reign even though the plot, assuming there was one, was ended three years later in 1645. *1666* No transition contest Sulayman: "The two most direct and available candidates for succession were Abbas II's two sons ... Safi was selected and his accession took place in November 1666".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_489_IrSafvd] *1688* No contested transition Soltan Hosein: "the eldest of Sulayman's seven sons ... acceded to the throne with the help of his aunt Maryam Bekum... The smoothness of Sultan Husayn's accession, like that of his father, underlines the degree to which, between and among each other, the realm's various key constituencies were still cooperating more than competing."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_490_IrSafvd]
